Prophethood
beginner
8 min
Five Features Distinguishing Prophetic Miracles from Sorcery
Maturidi enumerates five characteristics showing prophetic miracles are from God, not sorcery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five key distinctions Imam Maturidi draws between prophetic miracles and sorcery, and why these distinctions prove the prophets are sent by God.

Core Idea
Prophetic miracles are real, moral, and self-sacrificing; sorcery is illusory, self-serving, and temporary.

Imam Maturidi provides a clear framework to differentiate the miracles of prophets from the tricks of sorcerers. While both may appear extraordinary, their inner realities and purposes are fundamentally different. This passage lays out five criteria that show prophetic signs come from God alone.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the original Arabic and the English translation to get the full context of Maturidi's argument.
Note that the fifth point is incomplete due to a cut-off in the manuscript.
2
List the five distinguishing features
From the passage, extract each of the five features. Write them down in your own words.
The first three are clearer; the fourth and fifth involve moral obligations and self-sacrifice.
3
Compare prophetic miracles with sorcery
For each feature, note how Maturidi contrasts the prophetic miracle with sorcery. This contrast is key to his proof.
4
Reflect on the moral and sacrificial dimension
Notice that the prophets impose difficult duties (feature 4) and risk their lives (feature 5). Think about why this supports their truthfulness over sorcerers who seek worldly gain.
Sorcerers do not impose moral obligations or risk their lives for others.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما كان ما كان معهم معان يعلم بها أنهم مبعوثون أحدها ، أنها تخرج حقيقة تبقى ببقاء الخلقة ، والسحر هو شيء يأخذ البصر ثم يضمحل . والثانى ، أن آية الرسل تمنع أن يدعيها من لیس برسول ، فيتبقى معه إن كانت في جهة سحراً ، وما كان . والثالث ، أن أولئك الذين تكلفوا استخراج العجائب بالتعلم فهم قد مالوا إلى لو كان حقا لكان به غنى عن عرض الدنيا ، فكان الكذب . والرابع ، إن الرسل حملوا ما فى الأنفس إنكاره ، ذلك من كفها عن الملاذ والشهوات وحفظها عن عز الدنيا وشرفها ودعاء أمثالها إلى ترك ذلك لله . والخامس ، مخاطرتهم بالأنفس وبذلها في وقت ضعفهم وقلة أنصارهم من الخلق والتعرض الجبارين الذين بهم
Along with what they had, they have meanings by which it is known that they are sent: One of them is that they emerge as a reality that remains with the permanence of creation, while sorcery is something that takes the sight then vanishes. The second is that the sign of the messenger prevents anyone who is not a messenger from claiming it, so it would remain with him if it were in the direction of sorcery, but it was not. The third is that those who undertake to produce wonders by learning, they have inclined to [the idea that] if it were true, they would be independent of the pursuit of the world, so it would be false. The fourth is that the messengers imposed what is deniable in souls, that is, restraining them from pleasures and desires and preserving them from the might and honor of the world, and calling their likes to abandon that for Allah. The fifth is their risking their lives and sacrificing them in time of weakness and few supporters among creation, and exposing [themselves] to tyrants who...
Source pages p. 788

Commentary
This passage enumerates five distinguishing features (maʿānī) that prove prophets are sent by God, setting their miracles apart from sorcery. First, prophetic miracles are real and enduring, while sorcery is illusory and temporary. Second, prophetic miracles cannot be claimed by impostors, whereas sorcery can be learned by anyone. Third, those who pursue wonders through learning are motivated by worldly gain, but if their art were true, they would not need worldly wealth. Fourth, the prophets impose difficult moral obligations that go against human desires, showing their message is from God. Fifth, the prophets risk their lives and sacrifice themselves despite weakness and few supporters, unlike sorcerers who seek safety. The fifth point is cut off but clearly indicates self-sacrifice as evidence of truthfulness.

Key Terms
معان
حقيقة
سحر
بصر
ملاذ
شهوات
مخاطرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are just impressive feats like sorcery, differing only in degree.
Correction: Prophetic miracles have enduring reality, moral weight, and self-sacrifice, while sorcery is temporary, self-serving, and lacks moral demand.

Checkpoint
What is the first distinguishing feature of prophetic miracles according to Maturidi?
A) They are more powerful than any sorcery.
B) They emerge as a reality that remains, while sorcery vanishes.
C) They are performed only by prophets.
D) They are always accompanied by a message.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's five features show that prophetic miracles are enduring, exclusive, selfless, morally demanding, and personally costly—completely unlike sorcery.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the fourth feature: prophets call people to restrain from pleasures and desires and abandon worldly might for Allah. What particular pleasure or desire in your own life are you most reluctant to give up for Allah, and what does that reluctance reveal about your heart's true loyalty?
?
The fifth feature speaks of prophets risking their lives with few supporters. When have you ever risked your comfort or reputation for the sake of truth, and how does that compare to the sacrifice of the prophets? If you haven't, what holds you back?
?
The second feature says that a prophetic sign cannot be claimed by an imposter. In your own life, are there ways you have pretended to have spiritual authority or knowledge you don't have? How can you ensure your words and actions are aligned with truth, even when no one is watching?

Prophethood
beginner
4 min
Miracles as Divine Proofs of Prophethood
Maturidi enumerates the second proof of prophethood: miracles as signs beyond human learning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi argues that prophetic miracles are beyond human capacity and learning, thus serving as divine proofs.

Core Idea
Miracles prove prophethood because they exceed human learning and natural capacity.

Imam Maturidi presents the second proof of prophethood: miracles. He explains that true miracles are beyond the reach of human learning and natural ability. Moreover, the prophets' lack of upbringing in such arts shows that these signs come directly from God. This passage establishes the miraculous as a key indicator of divine mission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the key elements of the proof
The passage presents two main points: first, miracles are outside natural capacities and unattainable by learning; second, the prophets were not raised in those arts, showing the knowledge is from God.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: Why does the fact that prophets were not educated in the miraculous arts strengthen the proof? How does this show divine origin?
This connects to the learning objective.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm your grasp of the main idea.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني مجيء الآيات الخارجة عن طبائع أهل البصر في ذلك النوع الممتنعة عن أن يطمع في مثلها أو يبلغ بكنهها التعلم، مع ما لو احتمل أن يبلغ أحد ذلك بالتعلم والاجتهاد فإن الرسل بما نشأوا لا في ذلك، وربوا لا به يظهر أنهم استفادوه بالله، أكرمهم بذلك؛ لما يجعلهم أمناء على وجهه.
And the second [proof] is the coming of signs that are outside the natural capacities of people of insight in that kind, which are impossible to aspire to their like or for learning to reach their essence. [This is] along with the fact that if it were possible for someone to attain that through learning and effort, then the messengers—since they were not raised in that [field] nor educated in it—would appear to have acquired it from God, He honored them with that, making them trustees over His face.
Source pages p. 788

Commentary
This passage defines the second proof of prophethood: miracles. Maturidi states that miracles are events beyond the natural capacities of those who are insightful. They are such that no one can aspire to imitate them, nor can learning reach their true nature. He adds that even if one could theoretically attain such things through learning, the prophets were not raised in those arts nor educated in them, so it is clear they received this knowledge from God. Thus, God honored them by making them trustworthy bearers of His message.

Key Terms
آيات
معجزة
رسل
تعلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that prophets could have learned miraculous abilities through study and effort.
Correction: Maturidi argues that even if such learning were possible, the prophets were not raised in those arts, showing that their miracles come directly from God.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the second proof of prophethood?
A) The prophets' noble character
B) The coming of miracles outside natural capacities
C) The rational arguments
D) The Quran

Key Takeaway
Maturidi asserts that prophetic miracles are divine signs because they exceed human learning and the prophets' backgrounds confirm their divine origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a prophetic miracle as a metaphor or exaggeration because it seemed impossible? How does Maturidi's argument—that miracles are beyond human capacity and that the prophets' own background proves they didn't learn them—confront your tendency to reduce the miraculous to the ordinary?
?
The prophets were not raised in the arts they brought miracles in. In what area of your life are you trusting your own learning and effort instead of acknowledging that every good thing comes directly from God? What would it look like to attribute your successes to His favor rather than your skill?
?
If these signs are truly from God and beyond human reach, then what does that demand of you when you read the Quran today? Are you reading it as a book of human wisdom or as a divine message that requires your unconditional submission? Change one habit this week to treat it as the latter.

Prophethood
intermediate
8 min
Origin of Sorcery and First Two Marks of Prophethood
Context and first two distinctions between prophetic signs and sorcery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi distinguishes prophetic signs from sorcery through their permanence and exclusivity.

Core Idea
Prophetic signs endure; sorcery fades, and cannot be claimed by impostors.

Maturidi first notes that sorcery originated from heaven but was corrupted through learning. Then he presents two key differences: prophetic signs remain as lasting realities, while sorcery is deceptive and temporary; and a true sign prevents false claimants from imitating it. This lesson explores these foundational distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Maturidi connects the origin of sorcery to his first distinction.
The Arabic uses ‘آية’ for sign and ‘سحر’ for sorcery.
2
Understand the context: sorcery's origin
Maturidi acknowledges that sorcery originally came from heaven but was transmitted through learning, unlike prophetic signs which are given directly.
This sets the stage for the distinctions.
3
Identify the first mark: permanence
Prophetic signs emerge as a reality that lasts for the duration of creation, while sorcery captures the eyesight then vanishes.
The key contrast is ‘حقيقة تبقى’ vs. ‘يضمحل’.
4
Identify the second mark: exclusivity
The sign of a messenger prevents false claimants from imitating it; if someone tries, it remains as if sorcery but is not a true sign.
5
Reflect on the two marks together
These two marks show that prophetic signs are inherently distinct and superior, grounded in reality and divine authentication.
This lesson establishes a clear criterion for distinguishing truth from illusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهم أيضا معان فاقوا بها السحرة، على أن علم السحر أصله من السماء، لكن الناس نسوا أصله وتوارثوه بالتعلم، وكذلك المكاسب والحرف والصناعات كلها، فمن أكرم لا بالوجه الذي هو طريقه في المعارف عليم أن ذلك تخصيص الأمر عظيم. مع ما كان ما كان معهم معان يعلم بها أنهم مبعوثون أحدها، أنها تخرج حقيقة تبقى ببقاء الخلقة، والسحر هو شيء يأخذ البصر ثم يضمحل. والثاني، أن آية الرسل تمنع أن يدعيها من ليس برسول، فيتبقى معه إن كانت في جهة سحرا، وما كان. والثالث، أن أولئك الذين تكلفوا استخراج العجائب بالتعلم فهم قد مالوا إلى لو كان حقا لكان به غنى عن عرض الدنيا، فكان الكذب. والرابع، إن الرسل حملوا ما في الأنفس إنكاره، ذلك من كفها عن الملاذ والشهوات وحفظها عن عز الدنيا وشرفها ودعاء أمثالها إلى ترك ذلك لله. والخامس، مخاطرتهم بالأنفس وبذلها في وقت ضعفهم وقلة أنصارهم من الخلق والتعرض للجبارين الذين بهم تنغيص ما هم فيه عليهم، وإظهار القوة لهم من عند العزيز، على ما علموا من سوء صنيعهم بالمخالفين لهم وبخاصة من يخافون منهم. وتشتيت أمورهم.
And they also have meanings by which they surpass the sorcerers, despite the fact that the science of sorcery originated from the heavens, but people forgot its origin and inherited it through learning. Likewise are professions, crafts, and all industries. So whoever is honored not in the way that is the path to knowledge knows that that is a great specification. Along with that, there are meanings by which it is known that they are sent: one of them: that they [the signs] emerge as a reality that remains for the duration of creation, while sorcery is something that captures the eyesight then vanishes. The second: that the sign of the messenger prevents anyone who is not a messenger from claiming it, so it remains with him [the false claimant] as if it were sorcery in some respect, but it is not. The third: that those who undertake to produce wonders through learning have inclined to them; if it were true, they would have been satisfied with it instead of seeking worldly gain, so it is false. The fourth: that the messengers imposed what souls reject, that is, restraining them from pleasures and desires, preserving them from the honor and glory of this world, and calling others to abandon that for God. The fifth: their risking their lives and sacrificing them at times of their weakness and lack of supporters among creation, and exposing themselves to tyrants who cause distress upon them and show power against them from the Almighty, based on their knowledge of the evil deeds of those who oppose them, especially those they fear. And the scattering of their affairs.
Source pages p. 788

Commentary
This passage classifies five signs that prove prophethood and distinguish it from sorcery. Maturidi first notes that sorcery originally came from heaven but was forgotten and learned, whereas prophetic signs are not learned. Then he lists: (1) Prophetic signs remain as a reality lasting as long as creation, while sorcery is temporary and deceptive. (2) A prophetic sign prevents false claimants from imitating it. (3) Those who try to produce wonders through learning would be satisfied with worldly gain, but prophets do not seek that. (4) Prophets call people to self-restraint and away from worldly pleasures, which is against human inclination. (5) Prophets risk their lives even when weak and few in number, facing tyrants, and God supports them. These points show their truthfulness.

Key Terms
آية
سحر
رسول
حقيقة
اضمحلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People may think prophetic miracles and sorcery are essentially the same kind of phenomenon.
Correction: Maturidi shows that prophetic signs are permanent and exclusive to prophets, whereas sorcery is temporary and learnable.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the first distinguishing mark of a prophetic sign compared to sorcery?
A) It is learned from heaven
B) It remains as a reality for the duration of creation
C) It can be imitated by anyone
D) It vanishes quickly

Key Takeaway
Prophetic signs are distinguished by their permanence and exclusivity, unlike sorcery which is temporary and teachable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been captivated by something that seemed real but later proved to be an illusion? What does this lesson teach you about how to discern lasting truth from temporary deception?
?
The second mark says that no one can claim a prophetic sign falsely. How does this challenge you when you consider the sources of authority you trust in your own life? Are you willing to test them against this criterion?
?
This passage shows that even sorcery has a heavenly origin but was corrupted through human learning. Is there any good thing in your life that has been distorted by human tradition? What would it mean for you to return to its pure origin?

Prophethood
intermediate
8 min
The Last Three Marks: Motives, Self-Restraint, and Risk
Three further distinctions: motives, self-restraint, and risk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the remaining three distinctions Maturidi uses to prove prophethood: the inability of learners to produce true signs, the call to self-restraint, and the willingness to risk one's life.

Core Idea
Prophets seek no worldly gain, call to sacrifice, and risk their lives — proving their truthfulness.

Maturidi continues with three more marks that distinguish prophetic signs from sorcery. Those who produce wonders through learning would be satisfied with worldly gain, but prophets do not seek that. Instead, they call people to self-restraint and away from pleasures, and they risk their lives even when weak and outnumbered, facing tyrants. These points show their sincerity and divine support.

Guided Path
1
Read the third mark: learners' failure
Read the third distinction: those who try to produce wonders through learning would be satisfied with worldly gain, but prophetic signs are not for worldly profit.
The Arabic says: 'فهم قد مالوا إلى لو كان حقا لكان به غنى عن عرض الدنيا'.
2
Read the fourth mark: call to self-restraint
Prophets impose what souls reject: restraining from pleasures and desires, and calling others to abandon worldly honor for God.
This goes against natural human inclination.
3
Read the fifth mark: risking lives
Prophets risk their lives despite weakness and lack of supporters, facing tyrants, and God shows power through them.
This demonstrates reliance on divine support.
4
Reflect on the cumulative evidence
All three marks together show that prophetic signs are not learned tricks but genuine divine interventions accompanied by selfless motives and courage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهم أيضا معان فاقوا بها السحرة، على أن علم السحر أصله من السماء، لكن الناس نسوا أصله وتوارثوه بالتعلم، وكذلك المكاسب والحرف والصناعات كلها، فمن أكرم لا بالوجه الذي هو طريقه في المعارف عليم أن ذلك تخصيص الأمر عظيم. مع ما كان ما كان معهم معان يعلم بها أنهم مبعوثون أحدها، أنها تخرج حقيقة تبقى ببقاء الخلقة، والسحر هو شيء يأخذ البصر ثم يضمحل. والثاني، أن آية الرسل تمنع أن يدعيها من ليس برسول، فيتبقى معه إن كانت في جهة سحرا، وما كان. والثالث، أن أولئك الذين تكلفوا استخراج العجائب بالتعلم فهم قد مالوا إلى لو كان حقا لكان به غنى عن عرض الدنيا، فكان الكذب. والرابع، إن الرسل حملوا ما في الأنفس إنكاره، ذلك من كفها عن الملاذ والشهوات وحفظها عن عز الدنيا وشرفها ودعاء أمثالها إلى ترك ذلك لله. والخامس، مخاطرتهم بالأنفس وبذلها في وقت ضعفهم وقلة أنصارهم من الخلق والتعرض للجبارين الذين بهم تنغيص ما هم فيه عليهم، وإظهار القوة لهم من عند العزيز، على ما علموا من سوء صنيعهم بالمخالفين لهم وبخاصة من يخافون منهم. وتشتيت أمورهم.
And they also have meanings by which they surpass the sorcerers, despite the fact that the science of sorcery originated from the heavens, but people forgot its origin and inherited it through learning. Likewise are professions, crafts, and all industries. So whoever is honored not in the way that is the path to knowledge knows that that is a great specification. Along with that, there are meanings by which it is known that they are sent: one of them: that they [the signs] emerge as a reality that remains for the duration of creation, while sorcery is something that captures the eyesight then vanishes. The second: that the sign of the messenger prevents anyone who is not a messenger from claiming it, so it remains with him [the false claimant] as if it were sorcery in some respect, but it is not. The third: that those who undertake to produce wonders through learning have inclined to them; if it were true, they would have been satisfied with it instead of seeking worldly gain, so it is false. The fourth: that the messengers imposed what souls reject, that is, restraining them from pleasures and desires, preserving them from the honor and glory of this world, and calling others to abandon that for God. The fifth: their risking their lives and sacrificing them at times of their weakness and lack of supporters among creation, and exposing themselves to tyrants who cause distress upon them and show power against them from the Almighty, based on their knowledge of the evil deeds of those who oppose them, especially those they fear. And the scattering of their affairs.
Source pages p. 788

Commentary
This passage classifies five signs that prove prophethood and distinguish it from sorcery. Maturidi first notes that sorcery originally came from heaven but was forgotten and learned, whereas prophetic signs are not learned. Then he lists: (1) Prophetic signs remain as a reality lasting as long as creation, while sorcery is temporary and deceptive. (2) A prophetic sign prevents false claimants from imitating it. (3) Those who try to produce wonders through learning would be satisfied with worldly gain, but prophets do not seek that. (5) Prophets risk their lives even when weak and few in number, facing tyrants, and God supports them. These points show their truthfulness.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think prophets perform miracles for personal benefit or fame.
Correction: Maturidi argues that prophets neither seek worldly gain nor avoid hardship; they even risk their lives.

Checkpoint
Which mark shows that prophets do not seek worldly gain from their signs?
A) The first mark
B) The third mark
C) The fourth mark
D) The fifth mark

Key Takeaway
Prophetic signs are accompanied by selflessness, call to restraint, and willingness to sacrifice, proving their divine origin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which worldly honor or pleasure are you most attached to that you know you must abandon for Allah, yet you keep holding onto it? What would it cost you to let it go today?
?
If you were in a position of weakness and few supporters, would you still speak the truth against a tyrant? What would need to change in your heart to do so?
?
When you do good deeds, do you ever check your intention for hidden desire of recognition or reward? How can you purify it to be only for Allah?

Rational and Moral Proofs for Prophethood
Maturidi presents rational and moral proofs for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Maturidi argues for prophethood using both rational clarity and the perfect moral character of prophets.

Core Idea
Prophets combine rational truth with moral perfection.

Maturidi now provides additional proofs for prophethood, beginning with what is rationally evident and good in governance, then highlighting the prophets' flawless character, endurance, and selflessness. This lesson covers these two foundational arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two types of proof
The passage presents two distinct proofs: rational clarity and moral perfection. Find each one and notice how Maturidi describes them.
3
Reflect on the moral proof
Ask yourself why a prophet's perfect character is a strong evidence for prophethood. How does it distinguish them from impostors?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنهم إلى ما في العقول بيانه، وفي سياسات الملك حسنه، وبما في توقيف الخلق عليه صلاحهم دينا ودنيا، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا أنهم لم يقصروا في شيء دعوا إليه اجتهادا، ولا روي في شيء من أمورهم هوادة، ولا عرف في شيء من أخلاقهم نكير، ولا في شيء من الأسباب التي بكل واحد مما فيها بعد الناس بذلك ما يوصف بالتمام من السخاء والشجاعة ومكارم الأخلاق والرحمة بالخلق والإشفاق عليهم، وفي الزهادة في الدنيا وتحمل مؤن الخلق، وغير ذلك مما يحق الميل إلى كل من فيه خصلة منها والتعظيم له لمكان ذلك، فكيف لمن جمع الخصال الأداء المعروفة في المكارم مع حسن عن الله جل ثناؤه، والصبر له فيما يصيبهم من المكروه مما لا يحتمل أن يكون شيء من ذلك يحتمل، على تمكين الخلاص ببعض المداهنة. وفيهم أيضا وعد العواقب ورجوع الأمر إليهم، فخرج الأمر على ذلك. وفيهم أنه لم يذكر عن أحد نظر إليهم بعين التبجيل واستمع إليهم بالنصح لأنفسهم إلا أبصروا الحق في مقالتهم، ولا اتبعهم أحد فخالفهم إلا بعد العلم بإيثاره الدنيا على الآخرة والباطل على الحق. وكل الذي ذكرت كان لمحمد صلى الله عليه، مع غير ذلك من الآيات التي دامت له مما فيه إظهار نبوته، وأنه خاتم الأنبياء، منها هذا القرآن الذي تحدى به جميع الكفرة أن يأتوا بمثله وإن يعينهم على ذلك الجن والإنس، فما طمع في ذلك إلا سفيه أخرق هجره قومه لسخفه، وفيه أيضا بيان الحكم لجميع النوازل التي تحدث إلى يوم القيامة؛ ليعلم أنه جاء من عند من يعلم الغيب وما يكون أبدا، وبما جاء له من البشارات في فتح البلدان.
And also that they [the prophets] present what is clear in intellects and good in the policies of kings, and what in the instruction of creation brings about their well-being in religion and world, and there is no power except with God. And also that they did not fall short in anything they called to with effort, nor was any leniency reported in their affairs, nor was any blameworthy thing known in their morals, nor in any of the causes which, by each one of them, people are distanced from that which is described as perfect: generosity, courage, noble manners, mercy to creation, compassion upon them, asceticism in the world, bearing the burdens of creation, and other things that merit inclination towards anyone who has a trait of them and magnification of him because of that. So how about one who combines the well-known characteristics of noble qualities with goodness from God, may He be exalted, and patience with what befalls them of disliked things that cannot be borne while having the possibility of deliverance through some flattery? And among them also is the promise of outcomes and the return of authority to them, and the matter turned out accordingly. And among them is that it is not reported about anyone who looked at them with reverence and listened to them with sincere advice for themselves except that they saw the truth in their statements, and no one followed them except that they opposed them only after knowing that they preferred this world over the Hereafter and falsehood over truth. And all that I mentioned was for Muhammad, peace be upon him, along with other signs that remained for him that show his prophethood and that he is the seal of the prophets. Among them is this Qur'an with which he challenged all the disbelievers to produce something like it even if the jinn and humans helped them, so none aspired to that except a foolish, clumsy person whose people abandoned him due to his foolishness. And in it also is the explanation of the ruling for all events that occur until the Day of Resurrection, so that it may be known that it came from the One who knows the unseen and what will be forever, and by what came to him of the glad tidings of the conquest of lands.
Source pages p. 789

Commentary
This passage presents additional proofs for prophethood. Maturidi argues that prophets call to what is rationally clear and good in governance, and what brings well-being. They exhibit perfect character: they are generous, courageous, noble, merciful, and ascetic. They endure hardship without compromising. Moreover, history shows that those who sincerely followed them saw the truth, while those who opposed did so out of preference for this world. Then Maturidi specifically mentions Muhammad. His prophethood is proven by the Quran, which challenged all to produce its like and failed. The Quran also contains rulings for all future events, showing it comes from the All-Knowing. Finally, there are glad tidings of conquests.

Key Terms
عقل
سياسة
مكارم الأخلاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophethood can only be proven by miracles.
Correction: Maturidi shows that rational clarity and moral perfection are also strong proofs.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what kind of things do prophets call to?
A) Only supernatural miracles
B) What is clear in intellects and good in policies
C) Blind faith without reason
D) Asceticism alone

Key Takeaway
Prophets are proven by their rational message and their perfect moral character.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The prophets combined rational truth with moral perfection. In which area of your life do you separate intellect from ethics—and what would it cost you to integrate them as the prophets did?
?
You have seen that the prophets endured unbearable hardship without flattering for relief. When you face a difficult situation, do you compromise your values for comfort? What would it take for you to stand firm like the prophets?
?
The passage lists perfect traits like mercy, compassion, and bearing others' burdens. Which of these traits is most absent from your conduct, and what one concrete step can you take today to acquire it, seeking divine goodness?

Historical Evidence: Followers and Opponents of Prophets
Maturidi presents historical observations about followers and opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the behavior of followers and opponents as evidence for the truth of prophethood.

Core Idea
Sincere followers see truth; opponents prefer this world.

Maturidi continues his argument by pointing to historical patterns: those who approached the prophets with reverence and sincerity recognized the truth, while those who opposed them did so out of worldly preference. This lesson examines this social proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on followers and opponents
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the two groups Maturidi describes.
2
Note the two categories
The passage distinguishes between those who look with reverence and those who oppose. Identify the characteristics of each group.
The outcomes are also mentioned: promise of outcomes for prophets.
3
Consider why this pattern supports prophethood
Think about how the consistent behavior of sincere seekers and willful rejectors serves as evidence. What does it reveal about human nature and truth?
This connects to the learning objective.

The Quran: The Everlasting Miracle of Muhammad
Maturidi presents the Quran as proof of Muhammad's prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Quranic evidence for Muhammad's prophethood, including its inimitability and comprehensive guidance.

Core Idea
The Quran's challenge and comprehensive nature prove it is from Allah.

Maturidi now focuses on Prophet Muhammad, presenting the Quran as his enduring miracle. The Quran challenged all to produce its like and failed, and it contains rulings for all future events—showing divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about Muhammad's signs
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that it begins by connecting to the previous arguments before introducing new evidence.
2
Identify the two features of the Quran
The passage highlights two aspects: the challenge (تحدي) to produce its like, and the comprehensive rulings for all events. Find these in the text.
3
Understand why these prove prophethood
Consider how an impossible challenge and knowledge of the future point to divine origin. Also note the glad tidings of conquests as additional proof.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text

?
The glad tidings of conquests came true. What conquests is Allah promising you through His Book? Are you striving to make the Quran victorious in your own heart and home, or have you given up on that mission?

Prophethood
beginner
5 min
The Qur'an as the Prophet's Greatest Miracle
Maturidi presents the Qur'an as the Prophet's greatest miracle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi presents the Qur'an's inimitability as the primary evidence of Muhammad's prophethood.

Core Idea
The Qur'an's challenge proves prophethood by remaining unmet.

Maturidi begins his argument for prophethood by highlighting the Qur'an as the enduring miracle. He emphasizes that the Prophet challenged all of creation to produce its like, yet no one succeeded except a fool. This lesson explores that challenge and the Qur'an's comprehensive rulings as proof of divine origin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Qur'an challenge
Read the Arabic passage about the Qur'an's challenge. Notice the specific claim that even jinn and humans together cannot produce its like.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage contains two interrelated proofs: the challenge (tahaddi) and the Qur'an's comprehensive rulings for all future events.
The rulings indicate divine foreknowledge.
3
Reflect on the fool's description
Only a 'foolish, reckless person' would attempt the challenge. Why? Because the impossibility is obvious.
This underscores the miracle's clarity.
4
Connect to prophethood
These two aspects together prove that the Qur'an is from Allah and that Muhammad is His prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل الذي ذكرت كان لمحمد صلى الله عليه، مع غير ذلك من الآيات التي دامت له مما فيه إظهار نبوته، وأنه خاتم الأنبياء، منها هذا القرآن الذي تحدى به جميع الكفرة أن يأتوا بمثله وإن يعينهم على ذلك الجن والإنس، فما طمع في ذلك إلا سفيه أخرق هجره قومه لسخفه، وفيه أيضا بيان الحكم لجميع النوازل التي تحدث إلى يوم القيامة؛ ليعلم أنه جاء من عند من يعلم الغيب وما يكون أبدا، وبما جاء له من البشارات في فتح البلدان وإظهاره دينه بين أهل الأديان، وما فيه من الإنباء عما كان، مما يعلم الخلق أنه لم يختلف إلى أحد ممن يعلم ذلك، ولا نظر في كتاب قط، لتلك ما ذكر شأنه في الكتب السماوية حاج أهل الكتاب فلم يمكنهم إنكاره إشفاقا على أنفسهم، بل قد باهلهم مباهلته اليهود بقوله: فَتَمَنَّوا المَوْتَ، والنصارى بقوله: تَعَالَوْا نَدْعُ أَبْنَانَنَا وَأَبْنَاءَ كُم، والجميع بقوله: فَكِيدُونِي جَمِيعًا ثُمَّ لَا تُنْظِرُونَ، وإظهاره الأمن عنهم والثقة بالله بقوله: واللهُ يَعْصِمُكَ مِنَ النَّاسِ، مع ما له آيات في الخلق وهو النور الذي انتقل من ظهر إلى ظهر حتى خرج هو، وما كان من الخاتم بين كتفيه وما وصف بالربعة، ثم كان لا يزاحم طولين إلا فاقهما، ثم كان من السحاب الذي يظله أن يوحى إليه، ثم كان من شق بطنه وغسل ما فيه - معلوم ذلك - ورده معلوم ذلك إلى موضعه، ثم كان من هجر عبادة الأوثان في صغره، مع حرص قومه على ذلك، وما استقى به العباس فسقوا، ثم ما وصف من معاملته الكفرة أنه لم يكن يدارى ولا يمارى، ولم يكن فحاشا ولا صحابا، ثم ما لم يأخذوا عليه كذبا قط، وبذلك وصفه أعداؤه. ثم ما جاء من الآيات التي لما اختلفوا فيه فعرفوه بالسحر والكهانة والشعر ونحو ذلك، فما كان إلا لكثرة آياته، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And all that I mentioned was for Muhammad, peace be upon him, with other signs (ayat) that endured for him, manifesting his prophethood, and that he is the seal of the prophets. Among them is this Qur'an with which he challenged all the disbelievers to produce something like it, even if they were aided by jinn and humans. None aspired to that except a foolish, reckless person whom his people abandoned for his foolishness. In it also is the explanation of rulings for all events that occur until the Day of Resurrection, so that it may be known that he came from the One who knows the unseen and what will be forever. And among the glad tidings (bisharat) that came to him are the conquest of lands and the manifestation of his religion among the people of religions, and what is in it of information about what has passed, which the creation knows that he never went to anyone who knew that, nor did he ever look at a book. For that reason, what was mentioned of his description in the heavenly scriptures challenged the People of the Book, and they could not deny it out of fear for themselves. Rather, he challenged them with his mubahala: the Jews with His saying: 'Then wish for death,' and the Christians with His saying: 'Come, let us call our sons and your sons,' and all with His saying: 'So plot against me all together, then do not give me respite.' And he showed security from them and trust in God with His saying: 'And God will protect you from the people.' Along with the signs (ayat) in creation: the light that transferred from back to back until he emerged, and the seal between his shoulders and the description of moderate stature, and he would not rival two tall men except that he would surpass them, and the cloud that shaded him when revelation came to him, and the splitting of his belly and washing of its contents—known that—and returning that known to its place, and his abandonment of idol worship in his youth despite his people's eagerness for it, and the water from al-'Abbas that he drew and they were watered, and his described treatment of the disbelievers that he did not flatter nor dispute, nor was he foul or vulgar, and that they never caught him in a lie, and thus his enemies described him. Then came the signs (ayat) about which they differed, so they called them magic, soothsaying, poetry, and the like; that was only because of the abundance of his signs. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 789

Commentary
This passage is a cumulative argument for Muhammad's prophethood through multiple miracles. Maturidi first refers to previous points ("all that I mentioned") and adds further signs (ayat). He begins with the greatest miracle: the Qur'an, which challenges all creation to produce its like, a challenge no one could meet except a fool. The Qur'an also contains rulings for all future events, proving divine knowledge. He then mentions prophecies (bisharat) about conquests and the spread of Islam, and the fact that the Prophet had no human teacher nor studied scripture, yet the Qur'an contains accurate accounts of the past, which stumped the People of the Book. Further, the Prophet's mubahala (mutual cursing) with Jews and Christians, his trust in God's protection, and physical miracles (the light from generation to generation, the seal of prophethood, his stature, the shading cloud, the splitting of his chest, his purity from idolatry, the miracle of water from al-'Abbas, his impeccable character and truthfulness even by admission of enemies) are listed. Finally, the disbelievers, unable to deny the abundance of signs, resorted to calling them magic, soothsaying, or poetry. Maturidi concludes that this very abundance proves his prophethood.

Key Terms
القرآن
التحدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'an's inimitability is a subjective claim that can be debated artistically.
Correction: Maturidi presents it as an objective challenge that was never met, making it a factual proof of prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why did only a fool attempt to match the Qur'an?
A) Because the Qur'an was too long
B) Because it was impossible due to divine origin
C) Because they were not allowed
D) Because the Prophet forbade it

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's challenge and its comprehensive rulings prove it is from Allah, establishing Muhammad's prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'an, do you truly treat it as the unanswerable challenge it is, or have you become desensitized to its miraculous nature? What would change in your life if you lived with the constant awareness that it is the word of the All-Knowing?
?
The Qur'an claims to contain rulings for every event until the Day of Resurrection. Is there any area of your life where you are not seeking its guidance, relying instead on your own judgment or worldly systems? What would it cost you to submit that area completely to its authority?
?
Only a foolish person dared to challenge the Qur'an, and he was abandoned by his own people. When you encounter the Qur'an's commands, do you ever try to rationalize or negotiate your way out, effectively challenging it? How can you ensure you respond with complete surrender rather than pride?

Prophecies and the Challenge to the People of the Book
Maturidi invokes prophecies and mubahala as proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses prophecies of future events and the Prophet's courageous challenges (mubahala) as proofs of prophethood.

Core Idea
Prophecies of conquests and fearless challenges prove divine commission.

Maturidi continues his list of miracles by pointing to the glad tidings (bisharat) of future conquests and the spread of Islam, which the Prophet announced. He also highlights the Prophet's public challenges to the People of the Book, such as the mubahala (mutual cursing) with Jews and Christians, all of which required trust in Allah's protection. This lesson examines these events as evidence of prophethood.

Read the passage on prophecies and mubahala

This shows divine revelation.

The Prophet challenged Jews to wish for death (Surah 2:94) and Christians to mutual imprecation (Surah 3:61).
Such boldness indicates confidence in truth.
4

Verse 5:67 'And God will protect you from the people' gave the Prophet security.
This shows divine support.

Physical Signs (Ayat) and the Prophet's Impeccable Character
Maturidi enumerates physical and moral miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses physical miracles and the Prophet's untainted character as proofs of prophethood.

Core Idea
The Prophet's physical and moral traits confirm his prophethood.

Maturidi now turns to tangible signs (ayat) that accompanied the Prophet throughout his life. These include the transmission of the prophetic light through generations, the seal of prophethood between his shoulders, his ideal stature, the cloud that shaded him, the purification of his heart, his rejection of idolatry from childhood, a water miracle, his impeccable character, and his truthfulness acknowledged even by enemies. This lesson explores these signs as cumulative evidence of his prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of physical miracles
Note the light, seal, stature, cloud, chest splitting, and water miracle.
These are tangible events witnessed by others.
2
Consider the Prophet's childhood avoidance of idolatry
Despite his people's insistence, he never worshipped idols.
This shows innate purity.
3
Observe his character and truthfulness
Maturidi mentions he never flattered, disputed, or lied; even enemies testified to his honesty.
Character is a sign of prophethood.
4
Reflect on the disbelievers' reaction
Unable to deny the signs, they resorted to calling them magic or poetry.
The abundance of signs forced them to make excuses.

Al-Warraq's Objections to the Qur'an as Proof
Maturidi reports al-Warraq's first three objections against the Qur'an's miraculous nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Warraq's three objections against using the Qur'an as a proof for prophethood and why they are reported in Maturidi's work.

Core Idea
Al-Warraq's objections challenge the Qur'an's inimitability by questioning eloquence, distraction, and lack of reasoning.

In this passage, Maturidi reports the criticisms of a figure named al-Warraq against the argument that the Qur'an's miraculous nature proves Muhammad's prophethood. Al-Warraq raises four points, though only three are preserved here. Understanding these objections is crucial because Maturidi will later refute them. This lesson explores the first three objections as recorded in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of al-Warraq's objections. Notice the enumeration and the missing fourth point.
The text ends with 'والرابع' indicating the fourth objection is lost.
2
Identify each objection
List the three objections given and note how each attacks the argument from inimitability.
Objection 1: variation in eloquence; 2: distraction by war; 3: lack of speculative reasoning.
3
Consider the missing fourth
Note that the text breaks off before the fourth objection is stated. Reflect on how this affects the completeness of the argument.
The missing part is a reminder of the fragmentary nature of some texts.
4
Reflect on the context
Understand that Maturidi reports these objections to refute them later; he does not accept them as valid.
This is a common dialectical method in kalam: presenting the opponent's strongest case before rebutting it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم طعن الوراق المحتج بالقرآن بأوجه: أحدها تفاوتهم في البلاغة، ولعله تأليف أبلغهم. والثاني أن الحروب معه شغلتهم عن مثله. والثالث أنهم لم يكونوا أهل نظر ومعرفة، ألا ترى أنهم صدوا عن الإقرار مع توفر أسبابه عند أصحاب الضرورة، وعن النظر والمعرفة مع أسباب ذلك عند أصحاب الاكتساب. والرابع
Then al-Warraq criticized those who argue by the Qur'an on several aspects: first, their difference in eloquence (balaghah), and perhaps it is the composition (ta'lif) of the most eloquent among them. Second, that the wars with him occupied them from producing its like. Third, that they were not people of speculation (nazar) and knowledge (ma'rifah). Do you not see that they turned away from affirmation despite the availability of its causes among those of necessity (as'hab al-darurah), and from speculation and knowledge despite the causes of that among those of acquisition (as'hab al-iktisab)? And the fourth.
Source pages p. 790

Commentary
This passage presents the objections of a critic named al-Warraq against using the Qur'an as a proof for prophethood. Al-Warraq focuses on the argument that the Qur'an's inimitability proves Muhammad's prophethood, and he raises four counterpoints. The first objection suggests that the Quraysh's varying levels of eloquence might mean the Qur'an was actually composed by their most eloquent member. The second claims that the ongoing wars with the Prophet distracted them from attempting to produce a similar text. The third argues that the disbelievers were not people of speculative reasoning (nazar) and knowledge (ma'rifah), so their failure to produce a rival text does not prove inability; they often refuse affirmation even when evidence is clear (as with practical necessities) and neglect speculation despite accessible means. The fourth objection is not provided in this text, likely continuing on a missing page. Maturidi does not refute them here; he merely reports them.

Key Terms
الوراق
البلاغة
التأليف
النظر
المعرفة
أصحاب الضرورة
أصحاب الاكتساب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Maturidi endorses al-Warraq's objections because he reports them without immediate refutation.
Correction: Maturidi is a dialectician; he reports the strongest objections to refute them later, a common method in kalam.

Checkpoint
According to al-Warraq, what is the first reason that the disbelievers did not produce a text like the Qur'an?
A) They were too busy with wars.
B) They lacked eloquence.
C) They were not people of speculation and knowledge.
D) The Qur'an was composed by their most eloquent member.

Key Takeaway
Al-Warraq's three recorded objections challenge the Qur'an's inimitability by questioning the uniqueness of its eloquence, the distraction of war, and the disbelievers' lack of rational engagement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'an, do you find yourself secretly questioning its eloquence or being distracted by the 'wars' of your daily life? What would it take for you to set aside that distraction for just ten minutes today and listen to what Allah is saying to you?
?
Al-Warraq's third objection suggests that people can have clear evidence yet still turn away. Is there an area of your life—a command, a prohibition, a truth—where you have the evidence but you are deliberately ignoring it? What is the cost of your turning away?
?
If the Qur'an is truly the word of God, why do you not feel its weight in your heart when you recite it? Could it be that you have become like those who 'turn away from speculation and knowledge' even though the means are available? What will you change tonight so that this recitation is different?

The Prophetic Challenge and Divine Protection
Maturidi cites the Prophet's divine knowledge and mubahala challenges as proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Prophet's knowledge of the unseen and his daring challenges to the People of the Book serve as proofs of his prophethood.

Core Idea
The Prophet's bold challenges and God's protection are undeniable signs of his truth.

Maturidi opens his argument by highlighting a remarkable proof: the Prophet accurately reported past events without any human teacher, and then fearlessly challenged the People of the Book to invoke divine curses—knowing they could not accept. This lesson examines those challenges and the divine promise of protection that undergirded them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on knowledge and challenges
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how the Prophet's knowledge of past events is presented as a miracle, and then how he issued three distinct challenges to different groups.
2
Identify the three mubahala challenges
The passage mentions challenges to the Jews, Christians, and all disbelievers. Find each one and see how they differ.
The verses quoted are from the Quran, showing the Prophet's confidence.
3
Reflect on the protection verse
The Prophet declared his security from harm, trusting in Allah's protection. Consider how this strengthens the proof.
This shows his certainty and the truth of his message.
4
Connect to prophethood
Maturidi uses these facts to argue that the Prophet's knowledge and courage could only come from God. Ask yourself why this is a strong proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإظهاره دينه بين أهل الأديان، وما فيه من الإنباء عما كان، مما يعلم الخلق أنه لم يختلف إلى أحد ممن يعلم ذلك، ولا نظر في كتاب قط لتبقى له تلك ما ذكر شأنه في الكتب السماوية حاج أهل الكتاب فلم يمكنهم إنكاره إشفاقا على أنفسهم، بل قد باهلهم مباهلته اليهود بقوله: فَتَمَنَّوا المَوْتَ، والنصارى بقوله: تَعَالَوْا نَدْعُ أَبْنَانَنَا وَأَبْنَاءَ كُم، والجميع بقوله: فَكِيدُونِي جَمِيعًا ثُمَّ لَا تُنْظِرُونَ، وإظهاره الأمن عنهم والثقة بالله بقوله: واللهُ يَعْصِمُكَ مِنَ النَّاسِ، مع ما له آيات في الخلق وهو النور الذي انتقل من ظهر إلى ظهر حتى خرج هو، وما كان من الخاتم بين كتفيه وما وصف بالربعة، ثم كان لا يزاحم طولين إلا فاقهما، ثم كان من السحاب الذي يظله أن يوحى إليه، ثم كان من شق بطنه وغسل ما فيه - معلوم ذلك - ورده معلوم ذلك إلى موضعه، ثم كان من هجر عبادة الأوثان في صغره، مع حرص قومه على ذلك، وما استقى به العباس فسقوا، ثم ما وصف من معاملته الكفرة أنه لم يكن يدارى ولا يمارى، ولم يكن فحاشا ولا صحابا، ثم ما لم يأخذوا عليه كذبا قط، وبذلك وصفه أعداؤه. ثم ما جاء من الآيات التي لما اختلفوا فيه فعرفوه بالسحر والكهانة والشعر ونحو ذلك، فما كان إلا لكثرة آياته، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the manifestation of his religion among the people of religions, and what it contains of information about what was, such that creation knows that he did not consult anyone who knows that, nor did he ever look at a book so that it would remain for him that which was mentioned of his description in the heavenly books, which confounded the People of the Book so they could not deny it out of compassion for themselves. Rather, he challenged them with his challenge to the Jews by His saying: 'So wish for death' [al-Baqara 2:94], and to the Christians by His saying: 'Come, let us call our sons and your sons' [Aal Imran 3:61], and to all by His saying: 'So plot against me all together, then do not give me respite' [Hud 11:55], and his manifestation of security from them and trust in Allah by His saying: 'And Allah will protect you from the people' [al-Ma'ida 5:67]. Along with what signs he had in creation: he is the light that transferred from loin to loin until he came forth; the seal between his shoulders; his description as of medium stature; he did not rival two tall people but surpassed them; the cloud that shaded him when he received revelation; the splitting of his chest and washing of what was inside – known that – and returning it, known that, to its place; his abandonment of idol worship in his youth despite his people's eagerness for it; how he drew water for al-Abbas and they were watered; his dealings with the disbelievers as described: he was neither flattering nor disputatious, nor obscene nor abusive; they never caught him in a lie, and even his enemies described him thus. Then the signs that came about which they differed, recognizing him by sorcery, soothsaying, poetry, and the like – that was only due to the abundance of his signs. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 790

Commentary
This passage lists various proofs and miracles attesting to the prophethood of Muhammad. It begins by stating that the Prophet's religion was manifested among all peoples, and it contained information about past events that he could not have known without divine revelation. He never consulted a learned person nor read a book, yet he accurately described what was mentioned in previous scriptures, leaving the People of the Book unable to deny him out of self-preservation. The passage then recounts several explicit challenges (mubahala) that the Prophet issued: to the Jews to wish for death, to the Christians to invoke mutual imprecation, and to all disbelievers to plot against him together, demonstrating his trust in Allah. It also mentions the divine promise of protection. The text then enumerates physical and historical miracles: his pure lineage as the light transferred from loin to loin; the seal of prophethood between his shoulders; his medium stature that excelled others; the cloud that shaded him during revelation; the splitting of his chest and washing of his heart (a known event from his childhood); his abstention from idolatry despite pressure; the miracle of providing water for al-Abbas; his impeccable character, never flattering or disputing, never obscene, and never lying – a fact acknowledged even by his enemies. Finally, the passage notes that when the disbelievers saw his overwhelming signs, they attributed them to sorcery, soothsaying, or poetry, but this only reflects the abundance of his miracles. The passage concludes with the formula 'There is no power except with Allah.'

الإنباء
المباهلة
التحدي
العصمة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's knowledge of past events came from human teachers or books.
Correction: The text explicitly states he never consulted anyone nor looked at a book, so his knowledge was divine.

Checkpoint
What did the Prophet do to challenge the People of the Book that left them unable to deny him?
A) He debated them with logical arguments
B) He issued a challenge (mubahala) to invoke divine curse on liars
C) He fought them until they submitted
D) He bribed them with wealth

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's miraculous knowledge and fearless challenges, protected by Allah, are compelling proofs of his prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet challenged the People of the Book to wish for death if they were truthful. Are you living with such certainty in your beliefs that you would be willing to call upon Allah to expose any falsehood in your heart? What would that test reveal about your sincerity?
?
He declared 'And Allah will protect you from the people' and walked without fear. Where in your life are you hesitating to speak truth or act righteously because you fear people's reactions rather than trusting Allah's protection? Will you repent and rely on Him alone?
?
The Prophet had no human teacher, yet his knowledge was perfect. Do you seek knowledge from the same Divine Source through the Quran and Sunnah, or do you rely more on human opinions? How can you increase your direct connection to Allah's guidance today?

Core Idea
From the seal of prophethood to his perfect character, every sign confirms his truth.

These include his pure lineage, the seal between his shoulders, his physical beauty, the cloud shading him, the splitting of his chest, his early abandonment of idolatry, the miracle of water for al-Abbas, and his impeccable character that even his enemies acknowledged.

Al-Warraq's Four Objections to the Quran's Inimitability
Al-Warraq enumerates four objections to the Quran's inimitability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four objections raised by al-Warraq against the Quran's inimitability and their significance in the theological debate.

Core Idea
Al-Warraq's four objections challenge the Quran's divine origin by questioning eloquence, distraction, reflection, and prophethood.

Al-Warraq, an early critic, raised four distinct objections to the claim that the Quran is inimitable. These objections touch on eloquence, distraction by warfare, lack of reflection among Arabs, and the nature of prophethood. Understanding these objections is crucial to appreciating Maturidi's refutations.

Guided Path
1
Read the objections carefully
Read the passage where al-Warraq states his four objections. Note the logical structure: each objection starts with 'one of them is...'.
The objections are numbered in the text.
2
Identify each objection
List the four objections: (1) disparity in eloquence, (2) wars occupied them, (3) they were not reflective, (4) prophethood is a special distinction not requiring literary skill.
The fourth objection compares the Quran to prophethood as a unique gift.
3
Consider the plausibility
Think about why these objections might seem convincing to someone denying the Quran's divine origin. Reflect on the context of pre-Islamic Arabia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم طعن الوراق المحتج بالقرآن بأوجه: أحدها تفاوتهم في البلاغة، ولعله تأليف أبلغهم. والثاني أن الحروب معه شغلتهم عن مثله. والثالث أنهم لم يكونوا أهل نظر ومعرفة، ألا ترى أنهم صدوا عن الإقرار مع توفر أسبابه عند أصحاب الضرورة، وعن النظر والمعرفة مع أسباب ذلك عند أصحاب الاكتساب. والرابع لا يوجب ذلك له شيئا، فمثله النبوة: خصوص واحد بقوة من بين الجميع من غير أن يكون قدرتهم كانت بالفكر والتخيير، فلم يتكلفوا ذلك. فأما الأول فإنه لو كان ما قال يمتنعون عن ذلك بعد الجهد فدل تركهم طباعا. وأيضا أنه لو كان كذلك لم يحتمل مثله ممن يقول: اجتمعت الإنس والجن أن يكون أحد من البشر يبلغ علمه باللسان ذلك. والثالث أنه إذ نشأ بينهم، ومن عندهم عرف اللسان، فلولا أن له في ذلك من الله خصوصا لم يكن لغيره لا يحتمل أن يصير بهذا المحل. والرابع قد تكلفوا المجاوبات لأقوام معروفين في فن حتى اجتهدوا في قصيدة حولا، فلو كان يحتمل وسعهم يرجون البلوغ بطرق ما احتمل تركهم، وفي ذلك تشبيه على القوم، وقد بذلوا مهجهم ودنياهم في إطفاء هذا النور. والفصل الثاني لا يحتمل الذي ذكر؛ لما بذلك غنى لهم عن بذل المهج ولما أمهلوا قريبا من عشرين سنة قبل الحروب، ولما فيه تقريع الجن والإنس، وإنما جارب قوم. وبعد، فإن المحاربة لم تمنعهم من محاربات سمعوا من رسول الله كذلك القرآن لو احتمل وسعهم. والثالث لو كان كذلك لاستقبلوا بالإنكار والدفع، كفعل العرف، لا بالخضوع والامتناع. على أن العرب أذكى الناس عقلا وأشدهم حمية، وقد قاتلوا الشعراء بالأشعار أيضا. وبعد، فإن التقريع كان به جميع البشر والجن، وقد انتشر أمره وظهر في الآفاق. وأيضا فان الذي حمله على ذلك وما جاء به نشأ بينهم، وإن كان له، فذلك أيضا أنه له، ولا قوة إلا بالله معرفة ونظر مع نشوئه بينهم.
Then al-Warraq, who argued by the Qur'an, objected in several ways: First, their disparity in eloquence, and perhaps it is the composition of the most eloquent of them. Second, that wars with him occupied them from producing its like. Third, that they were not people of reflection and knowledge; do you not see that they turned away from acknowledgment despite the availability of its causes among those of necessity, and from reflection and knowledge despite the causes of that among those of acquisition. Fourth, that does not necessitate anything for him; the like of it is prophethood: a particular distinction by power from among all, without their ability being through thought and choice, so they did not undertake that. As for the first: if it were as he said, they would refrain from that after effort, so their abandonment indicates it [came] from nature. Also, if it were so, then the like of it from one who says: 'If mankind and jinn gathered' could not be such that any human would attain knowledge of the tongue to that extent. And the third: since he grew up among them, and from them he learned the tongue, were it not that he had from Allah a particular distinction, it would not be possible for anyone else to reach this status. The fourth: they undertook responses to known peoples in the art [of poetry] until they labored a whole year on a poem; so if it were possible within their capacity, they would hope to achieve it by some means, and they would not abandon it, and in that is a confusion upon the people, and they offered their lives and their world to extinguish this light. And the second category is not possible as he mentioned; because they would have thereby had no need to offer their lives, and because they were given respite nearly twenty years before the wars, and because it contains a rebuke of jinn and humans, and only [something] tried a people. Moreover, warfare did not prevent them from [engaging in] battles they heard from the Messenger of Allah; likewise the Qur'an, if it were within their capacity. And the third: if it were so, they would have met it with denial and repulsion, as is the custom, not with submission and cessation. Indeed, the Arabs were the smartest of people in intellect and the fiercest in zeal, and they also fought poets with poetry. Moreover, the rebuke was to all humans and jinn, and his affair spread and appeared in the horizons. Also, the one who brought it grew up among them, and if he has [this ability], that also is because he has [it], and there is no power except with Allah, [along with] knowledge and reflection with his upbringing among them.
Source pages p. 790

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes four objections by an opponent called al-Warraq against the Qur'an's inimitability (i'jaz). The first objection is that the Arabs varied in eloquence, so perhaps the Qur'an was composed by their most eloquent. Maturidi replies: if that were true, after effort they could have produced similar, but their failure shows it is beyond human nature. Furthermore, the Qur'anic challenge to all jinn and humans implies that no human can attain such eloquence without divine aid. The second objection: wars with the Prophet preoccupied them. Maturidi responds: they had twenty years of peace before war, so that excuse fails; also, the Qur'an rebukes all beings, which they would have tried to counter if possible. The third objection: the Arabs were not reflective. Maturidi counters: they were the most intelligent and zealous people; if it were possible, they would have rejected it outright, not just abstain. The fourth objection: prophethood is a special distinction, not a literary feat. Maturidi argues that the Prophet's upbringing among them without formal training, yet producing the miraculous speech, proves divine selection. He also notes that poets spent years composing verses, but the Arabs never attempted to rival the Qur'an despite offering their lives to extinguish its light. The passage ends with the conclusion that the Qur'an is from Allah, and human efforts cannot match it.

Key Terms
الوراق
البلاغة
التفاوت
التحدي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the four objections are all independent and equally valid.
Correction: Maturidi's refutations show they are flawed, but understanding them helps clarify the doctrine of i'jaz.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of al-Warraq's objections according to this passage?
A) Disparity in eloquence
B) Distraction by wars
C) The Qur'an is poetry
D) Lack of reflection

Key Takeaway
Al-Warraq's four objections question the Quran's inimitability from angles of eloquence, historical circumstance, intellectual capacity, and prophetic uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear objections to the Qur'an's divine origin like those of al-Warraq, do you feel a twinge of doubt? What would it take for you to be absolutely certain that this book could only come from Allah? Commit today to seeking that certainty through study and supplication.
?
Al-Warraq claims the Arabs were not reflective, but are you truly reflective about the Qur'an? When was the last time you sat with it not just for recitation, but to ponder its arguments and demands on your life? Will you set aside time this week to reflect on one verse as if your faith depended on it?
?
The fourth objection suggests the Qur'an is a special gift like prophethood—beyond human achievement. Do you secretly think you could have produced something similar if you tried? Humble yourself before the reality that this book is a direct sign from the Lord of the worlds. Will you let go of your pride and submit to its authority today?

Maturidi's Refutation of al-Warraq's Objections
Maturidi refutes al-Warraq's four objections in a mixed order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's key arguments refuting each of al-Warraq's four objections to the Quran's inimitability.

Core Idea
Maturidi refutes al-Warraq by showing the Arabs' failure despite their abilities and the divine nature of the Quran.

In response to al-Warraq's objections, Maturidi provides a series of refutations that demonstrate the impossibility of human authorship of the Quran. He uses logical arguments, historical evidence, and the concept of divine selection. This lesson walks through each refutation and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read Maturidi's responses
Read the passage from 'As for the first' to the end. Note that the refutations do not follow the order of the objections exactly.
The first refutation addresses the eloquence objection; then the third, fourth, second, and third again.
2
Identify the key arguments
For each refutation, identify the core argument: e.g., for the first, the Arabs' abandonment after effort shows it's from nature; for the second, they had two decades of peace; etc.
Pay special attention to the use of the Qur'anic challenge and the Arabs' own practices.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage ends with an affirmation that the Prophet's upbringing among them and his knowledge without formal training prove divine selection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم طعن الوراق المحتج بالقرآن بأوجه: أحدها تفاوتهم في البلاغة، ولعله تأليف أبلغهم. والثاني أن الحروب معه شغلتهم عن مثله. والثالث أنهم لم يكونوا أهل نظر ومعرفة، ألا ترى أنهم صدوا عن الإقرار مع توفر أسبابه عند أصحاب الضرورة، وعن النظر والمعرفة مع أسباب ذلك عند أصحاب الاكتساب. والرابع لا يوجب ذلك له شيئا، فمثله النبوة: خصوص واحد بقوة من بين الجميع من غير أن يكون قدرتهم كانت بالفكر والتخيير، فلم يتكلفوا ذلك. فأما الأول فإنه لو كان ما قال يمتنعون عن ذلك بعد الجهد فدل تركهم طباعا. وأيضا أنه لو كان كذلك لم يحتمل مثله ممن يقول: اجتمعت الإنس والجن أن يكون أحد من البشر يبلغ علمه باللسان ذلك. والثالث أنه إذ نشأ بينهم، ومن عندهم عرف اللسان، فلولا أن له في ذلك من الله خصوصا لم يكن لغيره لا يحتمل أن يصير بهذا المحل. والرابع قد تكلفوا المجاوبات لأقوام معروفين في فن حتى اجتهدوا في قصيدة حولا، فلو كان يحتمل وسعهم يرجون البلوغ بطرق ما احتمل تركهم، وفي ذلك تشبيه على القوم، وقد بذلوا مهجهم ودنياهم في إطفاء هذا النور. والفصل الثاني لا يحتمل الذي ذكر؛ لما بذلك غنى لهم عن بذل المهج ولما أمهلوا قريبا من عشرين سنة قبل الحروب، ولما فيه تقريع الجن والإنس، وإنما جارب قوم. وبعد، فإن المحاربة لم تمنعهم من محاربات سمعوا من رسول الله كذلك القرآن لو احتمل وسعهم. والثالث لو كان كذلك لاستقبلوا بالإنكار والدفع، كفعل العرف، لا بالخضوع والامتناع. على أن العرب أذكى الناس عقلا وأشدهم حمية، وقد قاتلوا الشعراء بالأشعار أيضا. وبعد، فإن التقريع كان به جميع البشر والجن، وقد انتشر أمره وظهر في الآفاق. وأيضا فان الذي حمله على ذلك وما جاء به نشأ بينهم، وإن كان له، فذلك أيضا أنه له، ولا قوة إلا بالله معرفة ونظر مع نشوئه بينهم.
Then al-Warraq, who argued by the Qur'an, objected in several ways: First, their disparity in eloquence, and perhaps it is the composition of the most eloquent of them. Second, that wars with him occupied them from producing its like. Third, that they were not people of reflection and knowledge; do you not see that they turned away from acknowledgment despite the availability of its causes among those of necessity, and from reflection and knowledge despite the causes of that among those of acquisition. Fourth, that does not necessitate anything for him; the like of it is prophethood: a particular distinction by power from among all, without their ability being through thought and choice, so they did not undertake that. As for the first: if it were as he said, they would refrain from that after effort, so their abandonment indicates it [came] from nature. Also, if it were so, then the like of it from one who says: 'If mankind and jinn gathered' could not be such that any human would attain knowledge of the tongue to that extent. And the third: since he grew up among them, and from them he learned the tongue, were it not that he had from Allah a particular distinction, it would not be possible for anyone else to reach this status. The fourth: they undertook responses to known peoples in the art [of poetry] until they labored a whole year on a poem; so if it were possible within their capacity, they would hope to achieve it by some means, and they would not abandon it, and in that is a confusion upon the people, and they offered their lives and their world to extinguish this light. And the second category is not possible as he mentioned; because they would have thereby had no need to offer their lives, and because they were given respite nearly twenty years before the wars, and because it contains a rebuke of jinn and humans, and only [something] tried a people. Moreover, warfare did not prevent them from [engaging in] battles they heard from the Messenger of Allah; likewise the Qur'an, if it were within their capacity. And the third: if it were so, they would have met it with denial and repulsion, as is the custom, not with submission and cessation. Indeed, the Arabs were the smartest of people in intellect and the fiercest in zeal, and they also fought poets with poetry. Moreover, the rebuke was to all humans and jinn, and his affair spread and appeared in the horizons. Also, the one who brought it grew up among them, and if he has [this ability], that also is because he has [it], and there is no power except with Allah, [along with] knowledge and reflection with his upbringing among them.
Source pages p. 790

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes four objections by an opponent called al-Warraq against the Qur'an's inimitability (i'jaz). The first objection is that the Arabs varied in eloquence, so perhaps the Qur'an was composed by their most eloquent. Maturidi replies: if that were true, after effort they could have produced similar, but their failure shows it is beyond human nature. Furthermore, the Qur'anic challenge to all jinn and humans implies that no human can attain such eloquence without divine aid. The second objection: wars with the Prophet preoccupied them. Maturidi responds: they had twenty years of peace before war, so that excuse fails; also, the Qur'an rebukes all beings, which they would have tried to counter if possible. The third objection: the Arabs were not reflective. Maturidi counters: they were the most intelligent and zealous people; if it were possible, they would have rejected it outright, not just abstain. The fourth objection: prophethood is a special distinction, not a literary feat. Maturidi argues that the Prophet's upbringing among them without formal training, yet producing the miraculous speech, proves divine selection. He also notes that poets spent years composing verses, but the Arabs never attempted to rival the Qur'an despite offering their lives to extinguish its light. The passage ends with the conclusion that the Qur'an is from Allah, and human efforts cannot match it.

Key Terms
البلاغة
التقريع
التحدي
النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi's refutations are purely theological without rational arguments.
Correction: Maturidi uses historical evidence and logical reasoning, e.g., the Arabs' known abilities and the timeline of events.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi refute the objection that the Arabs were not reflective people?
A) He denies the objection entirely
B) He says the Arabs were the smartest and would have rejected the Qur'an openly if it were human
C) He claims the Qur'an was revealed in a language they did not understand
D) He says they were too busy with trade to reflect

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's refutations demonstrate that the Quran's inimitability is proven by the Arabs' failure to produce a similar text despite their abilities and circumstances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi argues that the Arabs, despite their intelligence and zeal, submitted to the Quran's inimitability. Have you ever honestly examined the Quran's claim to be divine and concluded it is beyond human capacity? If not, what is holding you back from that conviction?
?
The Arabs were willing to sacrifice their lives to oppose the Prophet, yet they could not produce a single verse like the Quran. What does that say about your own attitude towards the Quran? Are you willing to let it change your life, or do you resist it like they did?
?
Maturidi highlights that the Prophet's untaught eloquence is a sign of divine selection. If you truly believe Muhammad was sent by Allah, what specific area of your life would you need to surrender to his message starting today?

Ibn al-Rawandi's Tawatur Argument for Prophethood
Maturidi reports Ibn al-Rawandi's tawatur argument for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn al-Rawandi's argument that tawatur (widespread transmission) compels acceptance of prophetic reports, based on the necessity of historical knowledge.

Core Idea
If you deny prophetic reports, you must deny all history — that is absurd.

Ibn al-Rawandi was a controversial figure who nonetheless offered a clever argument for prophethood. In this passage, Maturidi reports Ibn al-Rawandi's reasoning, which hinges on the principle of tawatur — the same mechanism by which we accept all historical knowledge. Read carefully to see how he forces the reader to accept revelation through the very same method we use to know the past.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the dilemma
Ibn al-Rawandi presents a forced choice: either no reports are reliable, or we accept tawatur reports. Notice the structure of 'either...or' (إما...أو).
This sets up the entire argument.
2
Notice the consequence of rejection
If we reject all reports, we lose all knowledge of the past, distant places, and events. Consider how much of what you 'know' comes from reports.
This consequence is absurd in practice.
3
See the conclusion
Since we do accept historical knowledge, we must accept prophetic reports transmitted via tawatur. The argument is that they stand on the same footing.
Maturidi will later refute this.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقاويل ابن الروندى فى الرسالة وبيان فسادها. قال الشيخ رحمه الله : احتج ابن الراوندى بما تقدم من الأغذية والسموم في إثبات الرسالة ، ثم قال : لا يخلو الأمر في الخبر إما أن لا يثبت البتة ، فيجب الجهل بالأيام الماضية والأماكن النائية والوقائع السالفة ، أو نقبل التواتر وما يضطر إليها فجب به اخبار الرسل ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
Ibn al-Rawandi's sayings in the treatise and the clarification of their corruption. The Shaykh, may God have mercy on him, said: Ibn al-Rawandi argued using what has been mentioned of foods and poisons in establishing prophethood. Then he said: The matter regarding report is not free: either it is not established at all, in which case ignorance of past days, distant places, and previous events becomes necessary; or we accept tawatur and what compels [knowledge] through it, and then the reports of messengers become necessary thereby. And there is no power but in God.
Source pages p. 792

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn al-Rawandi's argument from tawatur to defend prophethood. He argues that all historical knowledge relies on transmitted reports (tawatur). If we reject prophetic reports, we must also reject all historical knowledge, which is absurd. Therefore, we must accept the reports of prophets. Maturidi reports this argument to then refute it, as indicated by the heading 'clarification of their corruption'.

Key Terms
التواتر
الخبر
الرسالة
علم الضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophetic reports are less reliable than historical reports because they are religious.
Correction: Ibn al-Rawandi argues that both are based on tawatur, a widely transmitted report that compels knowledge necessarily.

Checkpoint
What is the core of Ibn al-Rawandi's argument in this passage?
A) Prophethood is impossible
B) Tawatur forces acceptance of prophetic reports
C) History cannot be known
D) Foods and poisons prove prophethood

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Rawandi argues that since we accept historical knowledge through tawatur, we must also accept prophetic reports transmitted via tawatur.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you accept the reports of the Prophet Muhammad with the same certainty you accept historical facts like the existence of ancient cities? If not, why? And what does that say about your faith?
?
If someone were to deny all reports except what they personally witnessed, would you consider them unreasonable? Then why do you sometimes doubt the prophetic reports that have reached you through tawatur?
?
The argument from tawatur forces you to either accept all reliable reports or be ignorant of the Day of Judgment. Are you willing to be ignorant of that Day? What steps will you take today to act on that knowledge?
