A) Truth and honesty
B) Lies and deception
C) Rational proof
D) Divine inspiration

Key Takeaway
The Quranic prophecies that came true are rational proofs that Muhammad's prophethood is from God, unlike soothsaying which is based on deception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states that the Prophet's message is 'constant truth over the course of days and time.' In your own life, do you experience the truth of Islam as consistently reliable? What area of your life have you not yet surrendered to that truth, and what is holding you back?
?
Imam Maturidi contrasts soothsaying (based on deception) with prophethood (based on truth from angels). Are there any sources of guidance or beliefs in your life that might be rooted in deception or illusion? How can you actively distinguish them from the truth of revelation?
?
The Quranic prophecies mentioned—such as the defeat of enemies and the preservation of the faith—were fulfilled precisely. How does knowing that God's promises never fail affect your confidence in the promises He makes to you personally (e.g., forgiveness, provision, victory over sin)? What one promise do you need to trust right now, even if circumstances seem contrary?

The Criterion for Prophethood Applies to Muhammad
Maturidi begins his refutation by demanding a consistent criterion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the same criterion used to accept previous prophets applies to Muhammad.

Core Idea
The meaning of prophethood is one; signs differ but the criterion is the same.

Maturidi addresses those who accept some prophets but reject Muhammad. He begins by asking them to identify the criterion they use to accept a prophet. If they point to any valid meaning that necessitates prophethood, then that same meaning applies to Muhammad. Even if the miraculous signs differ, the underlying meaning that makes a sign valid is consistent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once to get the overall flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the conditional structure: 'if they point to a meaning...'
2
Identify the two conditional arguments
Notice that Maturidi presents two scenarios: if they use a meaning-based criterion, and if they rely on apparent miracles.
In both cases, the conclusion supports Muhammad's prophethood.
3
Reflect on the consistency of the criterion
Consider why Maturidi insists that the meaning of a sign is unchanging even if the sign itself varies.
This is key to universalizing the criterion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول فيما بين المقرين بالرسل جملة والمنكرين لبعضهم على الإشارة أن نسألهم عن المعنى الذي له أقروا به أو أقرّ به سلفهم ، فإن أشاروا إلى معنى من المعاني التي توجب النبوة ، ألزمناهم في نبوة محمد صلى الله عليه ذلك المعنى بعينه ، وإن كانت الآية مختلفة بأنفسها ، فان المعنى الذي صارت الآية إنه غير مختلف ، وإن تعلقوا بظواهر الآيات لم يجدوا لأحد مثل الذي لمحمد عليه السلام في الأعجوبة والرفعة ، أو يخرجان على أمر واحد ، وإن ادعوا موافقتنا إياهم فان جواب ذلك يخرج من وجوه : أحدها يسأل عن علتهم قبل كوننا وظهور موافقتنا ، والثاني إنا قررنا بما ثبت لنا أن الذي أخبرنا بهم رسول ، وأنتم تنكرونه ، برهانكم؟ ، والثالث أن يقابلوا بالفرق الذي لم يقروا وما بما ادعوا ، والرابع أن يقال : إنما أقررنا نحن ممن قد أقر بنبوه نبينا ، فان كان من يدعونه هو ، فقد ثبتت نبوة نبينا ، وإن لم يكن هو ، فما الدلالة على نبوة محمد سقط دليلكم ، فها ممن ادعيتم له النبوة ليسلم لكم ما اردتم ، وبالله المعونة
Then the discourse concerning those who affirm the messengers in general but deny some of them: it is to ask them about the meaning by which they affirmed him or by which their predecessors affirmed him. If they point to a meaning from among the meanings that necessitate prophethood, we impose upon them that same meaning for the prophethood of Muhammad, peace be upon him. Even if the sign (āyah) differs in itself, the meaning that makes it a sign is not different. And if they cling to the apparent aspects of signs, they will not find for anyone the like of what Muhammad, peace be upon him, has in wonder and eminence, or they both come out on one matter. And if they claim that we agree with them, then the answer to that emerges from aspects: first, they are asked about their reason before our existence and the appearance of our agreement; second, we have established by what is proven to us that the one who informed us about them is a messenger, and you deny him—what is your proof?; third, they should be confronted with the distinction that they did not affirm and what they claimed; fourth, it is said: we only affirm from among those who have affirmed the prophethood of our prophet. So if the one they claim is he, then the prophethood of our prophet is established; and if he is not he, then what is the evidence for the prophethood of Muhammad? Your proof falls away. So here are those for whom you claimed prophethood, that they may concede what you desired. And with God is assistance.
Source pages p. 808

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of people who accept some prophets but reject others, particularly aimed at those who deny Muhammad's prophethood. The author presents a multi-step argument. First, he suggests asking them what criterion they use to accept a prophet. If they point to a specific meaning (e.g., miracles, moral excellence) that necessitates prophethood, then that same meaning applies to Muhammad. Even if the specific miraculous signs differ, the underlying meaning that makes a sign valid is the same. Second, if they rely on the apparent miracles, they will find that Muhammad's miracles are unsurpassed. Third, if they claim that Muslims agree with them (perhaps in accepting previous prophets), the author gives four responses: (1) ask them what their reason was before Muslims existed, implying their rejection of Muhammad is not based on reason; (2) we (Muslims) have established that the one who informed us about previous prophets (Muhammad) is himself a prophet—so denying him undermines their own acceptance; (3) they should be confronted with the inconsistency of accepting some prophets but not others; (4) our acceptance of prophets is based on accepting our own prophet; if the one they claim is indeed a prophet, then Muhammad's prophethood is also established (since he confirmed previous prophets), and if not, then they have no proof for Muhammad's prophethood. The passage ends with a supplication for divine assistance.

Key Terms
معنى النبوة
آية
أعجوبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Acceptance of different prophets can be based on different criteria or standards.
Correction: The meaning of prophethood is universal; if a criterion is valid for one, it applies to all true prophets.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about the meaning of a sign even if the sign itself differs?
A) The meaning changes with each sign
B) The meaning is not different
C) The meaning is irrelevant
D) The meaning is unknown

Key Takeaway
The same criterion that validates previous prophets equally validates Muhammad's prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own belief in Muhammad's prophethood, do you base it on the consistent criterion Maturidi describes, or do you rely on reasons you have never critically examined?
?
If you were to apply the same standard of evidence to other religious claims that you apply to Islam, would you find yourself accepting or rejecting them? What does that reveal about your intellectual honesty?
?
Consider someone who accepts previous prophets but denies Muhammad. According to Maturidi's argument, they either have no consistent criterion or must reject all prophets. Has your faith ever been tested by such a challenge? How would you respond if asked for the 'meaning' behind your belief?

Four Answers to the Claim of Agreement
Maturidi provides four responses to the claim of agreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's four logical responses to opponents who claim that Muslims agree with them in accepting prophets.

Core Idea
Agreement on previous prophets does not justify rejecting Muhammad; four responses refute the claim.

Some opponents argue that since Muslims accept previous prophets, they are inconsistent in demanding acceptance of Muhammad. Maturidi provides four definitive responses that systematically dismantle this argument, each targeting a different aspect of the claim, from the opponents' own reasoning to the foundational role of Muhammad in Muslim acceptance of prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with the four responses
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the enumeration of 'one', 'second', 'third', 'fourth'.
Each response is a distinct logical move.
2
Analyze each response's logic
For each of the four, summarize in your own words how it refutes the claim of agreement.
Pay attention to how the fourth response turns the tables.
3
Reflect on the overall strategy
Consider why Maturidi offers multiple responses rather than one.
Multiple angles strengthen the refutation.

Jesus' Miracles Do Not Prove Divinity: A Maturidi Refutation
Al-Maturidi refutes Christian claims by comparing Jesus' miracles to those of Moses and Ezekiel.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Maturidi refutes the Christian claim that Jesus' miracles prove his divine sonship by showing that other prophets performed similar or greater miracles.

Core Idea
Miracles alone do not prove divinity; other prophets performed the same or greater wonders.

This passage presents a dialogical refutation of Christian claims for Jesus' divinity based on his miracles. Imam al-Maturidi systematically counters each argument—whether it be Jesus' supplications, his weeping, his prayers, or his raising of the dead—by pointing to parallel or superior miracles in the lives of other prophets like Moses and Ezekiel. Understanding this argument helps clarify the Islamic stance on prophethood and miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the dialogical structure: each Christian claim is followed by a rebuttal.
2
Identify the Christian claims
Find the two main arguments: (1) Jesus' miracles through supplication, (2) raising the dead. Note how al-Maturidi responds to each.
The passage also addresses objections about weeping and teaching.
3
Analyze the comparison with other prophets
Al-Maturidi compares Jesus to Moses and Ezekiel. For each claim, observe how he shows that the other prophet did the same or greater.
Example: Moses also prayed and wept; Ezekiel raised a dead person; Moses turned a staff into a serpent.
4
Reflect on the nature of miracles
Consider why al-Maturidi argues that weeping and supplication are natural human acts, not signs of divinity. How does this strengthen his refutation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: من أن الله أظهر منه عجائب. قيل: وقد أظهر من موسى، فقولوا: هو ابن آخر. فإن زعمتم أن ذلك كان بدعاء وتضرع فمثله أمر غيره مع ما يحكى عن عيسى أنه كان يقول ليلة الأحد: اللهم إن كان من مشيئتك أن تصرف هذه الكأس المرة عن أحد فاصرفها عني. فإن قيل: كان عن عيسى البكاء والتضرع ليعلم الناس، قيل مثله من موسى. وبعد، فإنه وموسى: كانا يصليان نحو بيت المقدس ويتضرعان، ثم البكاء والتضرع فعل الطباع، لا يمتنع عنها، فما معنى التعليم؟ ثم إن استحق هو ذلك بالعمل لزم ذلك في موسى وغيره. فإن قيل: استحق ذلك بإحياء الموتى لا غير، قيل: قد أحيا حزقيل إنسانا. فإن عارض بالكثرة، قيل: اليهود يقولون موسى كان أكثر منه. قال الفقيه رحمه الله: وله أحيا عصا ميتة ثعبانا غير مرة، فهو أعظم.
He said: Because God showed wonders through him. It is said: And He showed through Moses, so say: He is another son. If you claim that that was through supplication and earnest prayer, then the case of another is similar, along with what is reported about Jesus that he used to say on Sunday night: 'O God, if it is Your will to turn this bitter cup away from someone, turn it away from me.' If it is said: Jesus' weeping and supplication were to teach people, it is said: The like is from Moses. Moreover, he and Moses used to pray toward the Holy House (Jerusalem) and supplicate; then weeping and supplication are acts of human nature, not impossible for it, so what is the meaning of teaching? Then if he merited that by action, then that follows for Moses and others. If it is said: He merited that only by raising the dead, it is said: Ezekiel raised a person. If he objects by [the claim of] greater frequency, it is said: The Jews say Moses had more [miracles]. The Faqih (may God have mercy on him) said: And he (Moses) revived a dead staff into a serpent more than once, so that is greater.
Source pages p. 810

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation of the Christian claim that Jesus is the Son of God because of his miracles. The author presents a dialogue: The Christian says that God showed wonders through Jesus. The response is that God also showed wonders through Moses, so they should call Moses a son as well. If the Christian objects that Jesus performed his miracles through supplication and tears (implying humility), the author points out that Jesus himself prayed in anguish ('take this cup from me') and that Moses also prayed and wept. Weeping and supplication are natural human acts, not signs of divinity. If the Christian says Jesus' tears were to teach people, the author replies that Moses did the same. Moreover, if Jesus merited sonship by his actions (i.e., his righteous deeds), then Moses and others who acted righteously would also be sons. If the Christian specifically points to raising the dead, the author replies that Ezekiel raised a dead person. If the Christian argues that Jesus raised many dead, the author counters that the Jews claim Moses performed even more miracles. Finally, the author notes that Moses turned a dead staff into a living serpent multiple times, which is a greater miracle (since it involved transforming an inanimate object into an animal). Thus, the argument that Jesus' miracles prove his divine sonship is refuted by comparison with other prophets.

Key Terms
عجائب (wonders/miracles)
دعاء وتضرع (supplication and earnest prayer)
إحياء الموتى (raising the dead)
حزقيل (Ezekiel)
عصا (staff)
ثعبان (serpent)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Jesus' miracles were unique in kind or degree, proving his divinity.
Correction: Al-Maturidi shows that other prophets performed similar or greater miracles, so miracles alone do not prove divinity.

Checkpoint
According to al-Maturidi, why does Jesus' weeping and supplication not prove his divinity?
A) Because it is a human act that other prophets also did.
B) Because Jesus wept out of fear.
C) Because weeping is a sin.
D) Because only divine beings do not weep.

Key Takeaway
Al-Maturidi shows that miracles are not proof of divinity, as other prophets performed the same or greater wonders.

God's Creation of All Acts Validates Prophethood
Maturidi argues that God's proof through prophets would be deficient if He did not create all acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why denying God's creation of all acts would undermine the proof of prophethood and make God's guidance dependent on creation.

Core Idea
If God didn't create all acts, His proofs would need creatures' help — impossible.

Maturidi extends his earlier argument about divine power to the institution of prophethood. If God were not the Creator of all human actions, including those of prophets and their opponents, then the miracles and signs that prove prophethood would be contingent on creatures' cooperation. This would imply a deficiency in God's proofs, which is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if He were not... then...'
This is a compressed argument; take time to see the logical flow.
2
Identify the implied conclusion
The passage presents an absurd consequence to prove a point. Identify what would be 'deficient and corrupted' and why that cannot be.
The conclusion is not stated explicitly but is clear: God must be the Creator of all acts.
3
Connect to prophethood
Reflect on why this argument specifically targets the proof (hujja) shown through messengers. What does it say about miracles and divine guidance?
This shows that prophethood itself depends on God's sole creative power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا أنه لو لم يكن خالقا لأفعال الخلق عامة [لما قدر على إظهار] من حجته التي أظهرها على أيدى رسله والتدبير الذي جرى عليه من أمر عالمة ما أنشأه خلقه إلى آخر ما ينتهى إليه أمره منتقصا فاسدا لولا مساعدة خلقه
And also, if He were not the Creator of the actions of creatures in general, [He would not be able to manifest] from His proof which He manifested through the hands of His messengers, and the management which proceeded from His command of His knowledgeable creation until the end to which His command terminates, deficient and corrupted unless by the assistance of His creation.
Source pages p. 832

Commentary
This fragmentary argument extends the previous reasoning to prophethood. If God did not create all human actions, then the miracles and signs He shows through His prophets would be contingent upon the cooperation of creatures. This would imply that God's proof (hujja) is deficient and dependent on others. Since God's proofs are perfect and self-sufficient, it must be that He is the Creator of all acts, including those of the messengers and their opponents.

Key Terms
حجة (hujja)
رسل (rusul)
تدبير (tadbir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: that prophets perform miracles by their own power, independent of God.
Correction: Maturidi shows that if that were true, God's proof would be deficient, because it would rely on creatures. Miracles are God's direct acts, proving His exclusive creativity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen to God's proof through prophets if He did not create all acts?
A) It would be more impressive
B) It would be deficient and require help from creation
C) It would be the same
D) Prophets would be independent

Key Takeaway
God's proofs through prophets would be imperfect unless He creates all human acts, so He must be the Creator of all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does knowing that even the proofs of prophets were entirely God’s act challenge your sense of self-sufficiency? What would change if you truly believed every good outcome is solely from God?
?
The passage argues that if God did not create all actions, His proof would be incomplete. Consider your own prayers and reliance on God: do you ever worry that your shortcomings might limit what God can do in your life? How does the absolute perfection of God’s power—described here—free you from that fear?
?
Think of a time you relied on someone else’s help to achieve a difficult task. Now reflect on the fact that the prophets, with all their miracles, were entirely dependent on God for every act. Are you willing to admit that even your best efforts and the help of others are nothing without God’s creation of them? What concrete step can you take today to live with that realization?

Refuting Objections to Divine Wisdom
Maturidi refutes claims that God's actions contradict His wisdom, using prophetic examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of two objections against divine wisdom based on prophetic history and the principle of clarification (bayān).

Core Idea
God's actions with prophets are always wise; denying this contradicts revealed clarification.

Maturidi addresses two common objections to God's wisdom. First, critics claim that God's treatment of prophets and their enemies seems unwise. Second, some argue that any practice not present in the Prophet's era is invalid. Maturidi refutes both by showing they contradict the very structure of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two objections Maturidi tackles.
2
Identify the two refutations
The first refutation deals with the claim that God's actions lack wisdom. The second refutation tackles the argument from prophetic tradition. See how Maturidi links the second to nullifying the Quran.
3
Reflect on the principle of bayān
Maturidi argues that if clarification cannot precede a conflict, then the entire Quran would be invalid. Understand why this is a powerful rebuttal.
Bayān means divine clarification or explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما قال من قبل الأنبياء فهو فيما أنا بهم موجود وفيما أبقى أعداءهم قائم ، ثم لم يخرج ذلك من الحكمة ، بل استدل إخوانه أن الذي يفعل هذا غير حكيم فما الذى يجيبهم فهو فى الأول جواب . وقوله : لم يكن في عهد رسول الله كذا ، فكأنه قال : لا يجوز ورود البيان فى الشيء قبل وقوعه ، وأن البيان لا يرد فيما لم يسبق فيه التنازع ، وذلك يدفع جميع آيات القرآن ، وما عليه الأمر المعتاد
And what He said about the prophets—that is, in what I am with them present and in what I leave their enemies persistent, then that does not depart from wisdom. Rather, his brethren argued that the one who does this is not wise; so what is it that answers them? It is in the first a response. And His saying: 'It was not so in the era of the Messenger of God'—it is as if he said: It is not permissible for clarification to come concerning a thing before its occurrence, and that clarification does not come concerning what has not been preceded by conflict; and that would nullify all the verses of the Quran and the customary state of affairs.
Source pages p. 845

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of arguments against divine wisdom based on prophetic history. Maturidi states that God's dealings with prophets (preserving them and leaving enemies persistent) are not outside wisdom, despite opponents claiming that such acts indicate lack of wisdom. The response is implied in the first part. Then he addresses another objection: that something was not done in the Prophet's era (implying it is therefore invalid). He counters that this would prevent clarification before an event, and would nullify the entire Quran because many verses were revealed before conflicts arose. Thus the objection is invalid.

Key Terms
حكمة
بيان
تنازع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a practice was not done in the Prophet's time, it is invalid.
Correction: Maturidi shows that this would nullify the Quran, which was revealed to clarify matters before conflicts arose.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the argument that something not present in the time of the Prophet is invalid?
A) Because the Prophet did not address all issues.
B) Because it would nullify the Quran, as many verses were revealed before conflicts arose.
C) Because such an argument is based on weak hadith.
D) Because the Companions had different practices.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes objections against divine wisdom by showing they contradict the Quranic pattern of revelation and clarification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly doubted God's wisdom when facing a difficult situation, thinking 'Why did He let this happen to me?' How does Maturidi's reminder that God's actions are never outside wisdom challenge you to change your heart right now?
?
Do you reject new ways of understanding or applying your faith simply because they weren't done in the Prophet's time? In what area of your life might this attitude be blocking you from receiving God's clarification today?
?
When you see the enemies of Islam seeming to persist, do you question God's justice? What would it mean for you to trust that His wisdom is at work even in what He leaves unpunished, and how can you actively cultivate that trust this week?

Who is More Praiseworthy? Prophet vs Iblis
Maturidi poses a comparative question on praise and obedience.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument about the relationship between power and action, and how it refutes the idea that mere capacity determines praise or blame.

Core Idea
Praise depends on actual use of power, not just hypothetical capacity.

Maturidi presents a striking comparison: a prophet who would sin if given the power, and Iblis who would obey if given the power. This raises the question of who is more praiseworthy. The answer reveals a crucial distinction between means (asbab) and the power that accompanies the act, and it has implications for how we understand divine justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the two-part question about praise and obedience.
The passage uses a rhetorical question format.
2
Identify the two comparisons
The first compares the prophet and Iblis on praiseworthiness; the second compares who is more obedient to Allah. Find the key terms: أَحمدُ, أطوع.
3
Understand the distinction in 'power'
The answer distinguishes between 'means' (الأسباب) and 'power that accompanies the act' (القوة التي معها الفعل). Notice how this affects the answer.
The same distinction is applied to water, knowledge, and good.
4
Reflect on the concluding phrase
The passage ends with 'ولا قوة إلا بالله'. How does this reinforce the argument?
It reminds that all power is from Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال: من أَحمدُ؟ من لو قدر على المعصية عصى، وهو النبي، أو من قدر على الطاعة أطاعه، وهو إبليس. قيل: إن عنيت الأسباب فالأول، وإن عنيت القوة التي معها الفعل أحلت، ومثله عليك فى الماء ومثله عليك في العلم والخير. ثم يقال له: من أطوع الله من لو والاه الله أطاعه، أو من لو عاداه عصاه؟ فبأى شيء يجيب فى ذلك فهو له فى الأول جواب، ولا قوة إلا بالله. وقال آخر: إنه لا عذر للعبد في الشاهد أعظم أن يقول لو قيل له: لم لا فعلت كذا؟ فيقول: لأنى لا أقدر عليه، فمثلا في الغائب. قيل: هذا يكون قادرا فيما يمنع عنه القدرة، لا فيها ضيعها باتا وما منع حدوث القدرة. وكذلك أيضا في الشاهد لا عذر أوسع من أن يقول: لم أعلم أمرك ولا نهيك.
And he said: Who is more praiseworthy? One who, if he had power to sin, would sin—and he is the prophet? Or one who, if he had power to obey, would obey—and he is Iblis? It was said: If you mean the means, then the first; if you mean the power which is with the act, you have made it invalid, and the same applies to you regarding water, and the same applies to you regarding knowledge and good. Then it is said to him: Who is more obedient to Allah: one whom if Allah befriended he would obey Him, or one whom if Allah showed enmity he would disobey Him? Whatever he answers in that, he has an answer for the first. And there is no power except with Allah. And another said: There is no greater excuse for the slave in the seen world than to say, when asked: Why did you not do such-and-such? He says: Because I am not able to do it. So likewise in the unseen. It was said: He would be able regarding what he is prevented from power, not regarding what he wasted entirely and what prevents the occurrence of power. And likewise in the seen world there is no broader excuse than to say: I did not know your command nor your prohibition.
Source pages p. 884

Commentary
This passage contains two related arguments. First, a question is posed: who is more praiseworthy (ahmad)? The prophet, who if given power to sin would sin (but he is given power to obey), or Iblis, who if given power to obey would obey (but he is given power to sin)? The answer distinguishes between the means (asbab) and the power concurrent with the act. If by 'power' we mean the means, then the prophet is more praiseworthy because he uses his means to obey. If we mean the power that accompanies the act, then the question is invalid. The same applies analogously to water, knowledge, and good. Second, a similar question about who is more obedient: the one who obeys when befriended by Allah, or the one who disobeys when shown enmity? The answer is consistent with the first. The passage ends with a discussion about excuses: in the seen world, the excuse 'I am not able' is accepted only if the inability is due to being prevented from power, not due to wasting it. Similarly, 'I did not know' is a broad excuse only in the seen world. The point is that in the unseen realm (gha'ib) with Allah, such excuses are not valid because Allah's power and knowledge are absolute.

Key Terms
أحمد
أطوع
القدرة
الأسباب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That having the capacity to do good automatically makes one deserving of praise, even if one does not use it.
Correction: Praise is based on actual use of power for good, not on hypothetical capacity. The prophet actually uses his power to obey, while Iblis uses his to disobey, so the prophet is more praiseworthy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if 'power' means the means (asbab), who is more praiseworthy?
A) The prophet
B) Iblis
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that praise is based on the actual use of power for obedience, not on hypothetical capacity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own obedience to Allah, do you measure yourself by what you actually do with the means you have, or by what you could do if you had more power? Are you content with merely imagining greater obedience while neglecting small acts of worship today? Ask Allah to grant you the sincerity to use every means He has given you for His sake, starting with the next prayer.
?
Consider Iblis: he would obey if given power, but in reality he disobeys. Do you see any similarity in your own life—promising obedience under hypothetical conditions while failing in actual duties? Tonight, before sleep, confess to Allah one specific area where you say 'I would if...' and commit to one concrete action you will take tomorrow, relying on His power, not your own.

The Excuse of Inability in the Seen and Unseen
Maturidi discusses the validity of excuses of inability and ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand when the excuse 'I am not able' or 'I did not know' is valid, and why such excuses do not apply to Allah in the unseen realm.

Core Idea
Excuses of inability are only valid if the inability is beyond one's control.

Maturidi turns to the nature of excuses. In the seen world, people often say 'I am not able' or 'I did not know' to justify failure. But are these always valid? The passage distinguishes between being prevented from power by Allah and wasting the power one had. The same logic applies to the unseen realm, but with a crucial difference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note that this is a new argument introduced by 'وقال آخر'.
This part is independent of the previous comparison.
2
Identify the two excuses discussed
The passage mentions two excuses: 'I am not able' and 'I did not know'. Note how each is evaluated.
3
Understand the condition for the excuse 'I am not able'
The excuse is valid only if the inability is due to Allah withholding power, not due to the person wasting their power.
The phrase 'ما منع حدوث القدرة' indicates prevention of the occurrence of power.
4
Consider the unseen realm (gha'ib)
The passage says 'فمثلا في الغائب' meaning the same applies in the unseen, but with Allah, these excuses are not accepted because His knowledge is absolute.
This refutes fatalism and human excuses before Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any claim of inability is a valid excuse before Allah.
Correction: The excuse is only valid if the inability is beyond one's control (i.e., Allah withheld power). If the person wasted their ability or failed to seek knowledge, the excuse is not accepted.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is the excuse 'I am not able' valid?
A) When the person really felt unable
B) When the inability is due to being prevented from power by Allah

Maturidi clarifies that excuses of inability or ignorance are only valid in the seen world under specific conditions; before Allah, the real cause is known.

?

?

?
Imagine standing before Allah and giving the excuse 'I didn't know'. Would it be true that you never had access to that knowledge? What step will you take this week to remove ignorance from an area of your life you've been avoiding?

The Objection of the Prophet's Pleasure with Those Who Fled
Maturidi answers an objection about the Prophet's pleasure with those who fled in battle.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi refutes the objection that the Prophet's pleasure with some companions who fled from battle implies that fleeing is acceptable to God.

Core Idea
Intention determines whether an action deserves divine pleasure or displeasure.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi addresses a theological objection about divine pleasure. The objection claims that since the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ was pleased with some companions who fled from battle, their act of fleeing must be something that God also approves. Maturidi rejects this reasoning by clarifying that pleasure and displeasure are tied to intention: the act of fleeing was not done for the sake of Allah or His Messenger, so it does not fall under divine pleasure or displeasure in that sense.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the concise structure: first the objection, then Maturidi's refutation.
The phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' is a humble closing.
2
Understand the objection
The objection uses the Prophet's pleasure as evidence that fleeing is acceptable. Consider why this might seem plausible to some.
The objection is based on an external observation of the Prophet's attitude.
3
Grasp Maturidi's refutation
Maturidi says the act was not done 'for Allah or His Messenger', so it is not evaluated as an act of obedience or disobedience in that context. Pleasure/displeasure pertain to acts done for God.
Intention (niyyah) is key in Islamic theology.
4
Reflect on the principle
Reflect on how intention shapes moral and theological judgment. This refutes the objection by distinguishing the act from the agent's overall standing.
5
Apply to similar objections
Consider other objections about divine pleasure based on actions of prophets or companions. How does Maturidi's principle help?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عارض بأن رسول الله رضى منهم، وذلك فاسد؛ لأن الانهزام منهم لم يكن لرسول الله أو لله، فيتكلم فيه بالرضا وغير الرضا، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then he objected that the Messenger of God was pleased with them. And that is invalid because their fleeing was not for the Messenger of God or for God, so one speaks about it in terms of pleasure and displeasure. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 898

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objection claims that since the Prophet was pleased with some companions who fled from battle, then fleeing must be something God approves. Maturidi rejects this reasoning because the act of fleeing was not done for the sake of Allah or His Messenger; it was a human weakness. Pleasure and displeasure are judged based on the intention behind the act. Since their intention was not for God or His Messenger, the act is not something that invites divine pleasure, and the objection fails.

Key Terms
رضا
سخط
انهزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the Prophet was pleased with a person, then every action of that person is approved by God.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that pleasure is based on intention; the act of fleeing was not done for Allah or His Messenger, so it does not entail divine displeasure or pleasure on that basis.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi reject the objection about the Prophet being pleased with those who fled?
A) Because the Prophet could not be pleased with disobedience.
B) Because the companions who fled were hypocrites.
C) Because the act of fleeing was not done for Allah or His Messenger.
D) Because God's pleasure is hidden.

Key Takeaway
Pleasure and displeasure are based on the intention behind an action, not merely on the outward act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you do a good deed that is not purely for God, do you assume God is pleased with it? What does this lesson teach you about the difference between an act's outward form and its intention? Think of one act you did today that was mixed in intention—will you repent and recommit to sincerity?
?
The objection assumed the Prophet's pleasure validated the fleeing. In your life, do you ever use the Prophet's mercy or love as an excuse for a sinful habit? Confront one action you justify in this way and ask: is this action truly for God and His Messenger? What will you do about it today?
